<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41586"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequence of the ultrasmall unicellular red alga Cyanidioschyzon merolae 10D"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature 2004 428:6983"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2004 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Small, compact genomes of ultrasmall unicellular algae provide information on the basic and essential genes that support the lives of photosynthetic eukaryotes, including higher plants1,2. Here we report the 16,520,305-base-pair sequence of the 20 chromosomes of the unicellular red alga Cyanidioschyzon merolae 10D as the first complete algal genome. We identified 5,331 genes in total, of which at least 86.3% were expressed. Unique characteristics of this genomic structure include: a lack of introns in all but 26 genes; only three copies of ribosomal DNA units that maintain the nucleolus; and two dynamin genes that are involved only in the division of mitochondria and plastids. The conserved mosaic origin of Calvin cycle enzymes in this red alga and in green plants supports the hypothesis of the existence of single primary plastid endosymbiosis. The lack of a myosin gene, in addition to the unexpressed actin gene, suggests a simpler system of cytokinesis. These results indicate that the C. merolae genome provides a model system with a simple gene composition for studying the origin, evolution and fundamental mechanisms of eukaryotic cells."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Small, compact genomes of ultrasmall unicellular algae provide information on the basic and essential genes that support the lives of photosynthetic eukaryotes, including higher plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Kuroiwa, T. The primitive red algae: Cyanidium caldarium and Cyanidioschyzon merolae as model system for investigating the dividing apparatus of mitochondria and plastids. Bioessays 20, 344–354 (1998)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d3545e999">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Kuroiwa, T. et al. The division apparatus of plastids and mitochondria. Int. Rev. Cytol. 181, 1–41 (1998)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1002">2</a></sup>. Here we report the 16,520,305-base-pair sequence of the 20 chromosomes of the unicellular red alga <i>Cyanidioschyzon merolae</i> 10D as the first complete algal genome. We identified 5,331 genes in total, of which at least 86.3% were expressed. Unique characteristics of this genomic structure include: a lack of introns in all but 26 genes; only three copies of ribosomal DNA units that maintain the nucleolus; and two dynamin genes that are involved only in the division of mitochondria and plastids. The conserved mosaic origin of Calvin cycle enzymes in this red alga and in green plants supports the hypothesis of the existence of single primary plastid endosymbiosis. The lack of a myosin gene, in addition to the unexpressed actin gene, suggests a simpler system of cytokinesis. These results indicate that the <i>C. merolae</i> genome provides a model system with a simple gene composition for studying the origin, evolution and fundamental mechanisms of eukaryotic cells.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p><i>C. merolae</i> is a small (2 µm diameter) unicellular organism that inhabits sulphate-rich hot springs (pH 1.5, 45 °C) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). The cells of <i>C. merolae</i> offer unique advantages for studies of mitochondrial and plastid (chloroplast) divisions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Kuroiwa, T. The primitive red algae: Cyanidium caldarium and Cyanidioschyzon merolae as model system for investigating the dividing apparatus of mitochondria and plastids. Bioessays 20, 344–354 (1998)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1046">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Kuroiwa, T. et al. The division apparatus of plastids and mitochondria. Int. Rev. Cytol. 181, 1–41 (1998)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1049">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="McFadden, G. I. &amp; Ralph, S. A. Dynamin: the endosymbiosis ring of power. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 100, 3557–3559 (2003)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1052">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Surridge, C. Ancient rings. Nature 422, 275 (2003)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1055">4</a></sup> because they do not have a rigid cell wall and contain just one nucleus, one mitochondrion and one plastid, divisions of which can be highly synchronized by light/dark cycles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Terui, S., Suzuki, K., Takahiashi, H., Itoh, R. &amp; Kuroiwa, T. High synchronization of chloroplast division in the ultramicro-alga Cyanidioschyzon merolae by treatment with both light and aphidicolin. J. Phycol. 31, 958–961 (1995)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1059">5</a></sup>. This alga also has the smallest genome of all photosynthetic eukaryotes, and contains a minimal set of small membrane-bounded compartments; for example, a microbody (peroxisome), a single Golgi apparatus with two cisternae, coated vesicles, a single endoplasmic reticulum, and a few lysosome-like structures, as well as a small volume of cytosol (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Kuroiwa, T. et al. Comparison of ultrastructures between the ultra-small eukaryote Cyanidioschyzon merolae and Cyanidium caldarium. Cytologia (Tokyo) 59, 149–158 (1994)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1067">6</a></sup>. One of the main points of interest that we discuss regarding this alga focuses on the origin, evolution and fundamental traits (for example, multiplication and differentiation) of single- as well as double-membrane-bounded organelles in plant cells. <i>C. merolae</i>, with its complete genomic information, provides an excellent opportunity for addressing such basic questions using microarray and proteome analyses. In addition, from an evolutionary perspective, <i>C. merolae</i> has other noteworthy properties that allow us to study the origin of eukaryotic cells, primary endosymbiosis between cyanobacteria and eukaryotic hosts, and secondary endosymbiosis between red algae and their hosts.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="The unicellular red alga C. merolae 10D."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: The unicellular red alga <i>C. merolae</i> 10D.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature02398/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature02398/MediaObjects/41586_2004_Article_BFnature02398_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature02398/MediaObjects/41586_2004_Article_BFnature02398_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="600" height="950" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Phase contrast-fluorescent images of the interphase (<b>a</b>) and dividing (<b>b</b>) cells show localization of nuclear (top), mitochondrial (middle) and plastid DNA (bottom in blue/white) after DAPI staining. The plastids emit red autofluorescence. The schematic dividing cell (<b>c</b>) contains a nucleus, a V-shaped mitochondrion, a dumb-bell-shaped plastid, a microbody and a Golgi apparatus, divisions of which can be highly synchronized by light/dark cycles.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature02398/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Samples of <i>C. merolae</i> 10D were isolated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Toda, K., Takahashi, H., Itoh, R. &amp; Kuroiwa, T. DNA contents of cell nuclei in two Cyanidiophyceae: Cyanidioschyzon merolae and Cyanidium caldarium Forma A. Cytologia (Tokyo) 60, 183–188 (1995)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1116">7</a></sup> from the hot spring algal collection provided by G. Pinto (Naples University). The entire <i>C. merolae</i> genome was sequenced using the random sequencing method (see Methods). We obtained 16,520,305 base pairs (bp; approximately 99.98% of the estimated total length) of the nuclear genome sequence (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#Tab1">Table 1</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 1</a>) with 46 gaps. The genome is distributed among the 20 chromosomes and ranges in size from approximately 0.42 to 1.62 Mb. No significant deviation in statistical parameters, such as base composition and gene density, were observed among the chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 1</a>). The overall G + C composition was 55.0%. The dinucleotide CpG in the <i>C. merolae</i> genome was exceptionally over-represented (1.151) compared with the expected value from observations of G + C content; it is generally underrepresented in other eukaryote genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#Tab1">Table 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Representation of C. merolae chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Representation of <i>C. merolae</i> chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature02398/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature02398/MediaObjects/41586_2004_Article_BFnature02398_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature02398/MediaObjects/41586_2004_Article_BFnature02398_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="806" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Chromosome 1 and mapping of the BAC clones (50 kb and 100 kb). The chromosome is represented as a bar with pseudo-colour assignments of local G + C contents. Side rods represent genes and gene-like elements (left for transcription towards the top, and right for the bottom). Telomere and subtelomeric elements are indicated respectively as semicircles and purple rectangles at each end of the chromosome. <b>b</b>, A bird's-eye view of 20 chromosomes showing G + C contents, genes, subtelomeric elements (designations on the side: P, H, L, A, E, and so on), and RNA genes. A putative centromeric (A + T-rich) region is located on each chromosome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature02398/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Nuclear and organellar genomes of <i>C. merolae</i> and comparison to other genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nature02398/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The putative repeat unit of telomeres in <i>C. merolae</i> is GGGGGGAAT, and as far as could be determined experimentally, it is found on both ends of the chromosomes. In addition, several sequence elements up to 20 kilobases (kb) in length were duplicated in 30 of the 40 putative subtelomeric regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). Each chromosome has, in varying degrees, a single A + T-rich region on its mid-section. As chromosomal centromeric regions generally have a biased base composition, this A + T-rich region possibly defines centromeres (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). The centromeres were confirmed via immunological experiments using antibodies against CENP-A, which was identified in the <i>C. merolae</i> genome (data not shown). Unlike many other eukaryotes, the <i>C. merolae</i> genome does not contain tandem repeated arrays of ribosomal RNA (rRNA) genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). A single rRNA gene unit (18S-5.8S-28S) was discovered on three separate loci. The three units were virtually identical in sequence. Moreover, <i>C. merolae</i> has only three copies of the 5S rRNA gene, the sequences of which are also almost identical. Therefore, <i>C. merolae</i> has the smallest set of rRNA genes among all eukaryotes thus far studied. These results might be related to the existence of a single small nucleolus without nucleolus-associated chromatin. Furthermore, they also promote studies on the origin and formation of the nucleolus, because even prokaryotic cells with more than three copies of rRNA gene units do not have a nucleolus.</p><p>A full-length complementary DNA (cDNA) library was used to map expressed genes within the <i>C. merolae</i> genome. Fortunately, 99.85% of the expressed sequence tags (ESTs) were mapped on the genome sequence. In addition, many cDNA clones encoded a single open reading frame (ORF) bridging both end sequences. This suggests that most <i>C. merolae</i> genes lack introns. The predicted genes were automatically annotated using several databases (see Methods and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a>). As a result, 5,331 genes were identified, and 86.3% of them had corresponding ESTs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>). The number of genes in the <i>C. merolae</i> genome is similar to those found in yeasts and malarial parasites, despite the great ecological differences between these species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Furthermore, the genes of the <i>C. merolae</i> genome are remarkable for their paucity of introns. Only 26 genes (0.5% of the protein genes) contained introns, and all but one of them had only a single intron. These introns had strict consensus sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 3</a>).</p><p><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#Fig3">Figure 3</a> summarizes the repertoire of <i>C. merolae</i> proteins on the basis of their assignment to eukaryotic clusters of orthologous groups (KOGs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Tatusov, R. L. et al. The COG database: an updated version includes eukaryotes. BMC Bioinform. 4, 41 (2003)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1267">8</a></sup>. Of the 4,771 predicted proteins, 2,536 were assigned to KOGs, by emulating the NCBI KOGnitor service (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/COG/new/kognitor.html">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/COG/new/kognitor.html</a>). The distribution of the functional classification of <i>C. merolae</i> was compared with those of other free-living unicellular eukaryotes, such as <i>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Goffeau, A. et al. Life with 6000 genes. Science 274, 546, 563–567 (1996)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1284">9</a></sup> and <i>Schizosaccharomyces pombe</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Wood, V. et al. The genome sequence of Schizosaccharomyces pombe. Nature 415, 871–880 (2002)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1290">10</a></sup>, and a higher plant <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Arabidopsis Genome Initiative, Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1296">11</a></sup>. The distribution was on the whole similar to both yeasts which have similar genome size although <i>C. merolae</i> cells contain plastids. The lowered proportion of genes for ‘secondary metabolites biosynthesis, transport and catabolism’ found in these unicellular organisms, as compared with that of <i>A. thaliana</i>, might reflect their simple cellular organizations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Comparison of the functional classification of C. merolae proteins with other organisms."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Comparison of the functional classification of <i>C. merolae</i> proteins with other organisms.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature02398/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature02398/MediaObjects/41586_2004_Article_BFnature02398_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnature02398/MediaObjects/41586_2004_Article_BFnature02398_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="600" height="477" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Columns represent the proportion of proteins assigned to KOG classification of each organism: <i>C. merolae</i>, <i>S. cerevisiae</i>, <i>S. pombe</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> in a left-to-right fashion. The actual numbers of proteins assigned to each classification are given in Supplementary Table 3.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nature02398/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In <i>C. merolae</i>, the division of double-membrane-bounded mitochondria and plastids involves a dynamic trio: an FtsZ ring of bacterial origin, electron dense mitochondrial/plastid dividing rings (MD and PD rings), and eukaryotic mechanochemical dynamin rings. Four genes representing mitochondrial FtsZ (<i>FtsZ2-1</i> and <i>FtsZ2-2</i>) and plastid FtsZ (<i>FtsZ1-1</i> and <i>FtsZ1-2</i>) were identified<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Takahara, M. et al. A putative mitochondrial ftsZ gene is present in the unicellular primitive red alga Cyanidioschyzon merolae. Mol. Gen. Genet. 264, 452–460 (2000)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1368">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Miyagishima, S., Nishida, K. &amp; Kuroiwa, T. An evolutionary puzzle: chloroplast and mitochondrial division rings. Trends Plant Sci. 8, 432–438 (2003)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1371">13</a></sup>. A large gene family consisting of more than 10 members encoding functionally diverse dynamins with a wide range of membrane pinching roles have been found in other organisms; however, only two dynamin genes (<i>C. merolae Dnm1</i> and <i>Dnm2</i>) are found, with a role in the later stages of the mitochondrion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nishida, K. et al. Dynamic recruitment of dynamin for final mitochondrial severance in a primitive red alga. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 100, 2146–2151 (2003)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1381">14</a></sup> and plastid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Miyagishima, S., Nishida, K. &amp; Kuroiwa, T. An evolutionary puzzle: chloroplast and mitochondrial division rings. Trends Plant Sci. 8, 432–438 (2003)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1385">13</a></sup> division, respectively. These findings suggest that plastids and mitochondria divide in a similar way, using very common systems consisting of the amalgamation of bacterial and eukaryotic rings. The dynamic trio of plastid division is conserved in lower algae to higher plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Kuroiwa, H., Mori, T., Takahara, M., Miyagishima, S. &amp; Kuroiwa, T. Chloroplast division machinery as revealed by immunofluorescence and electron microscopy. Planta 215, 185–190 (2002)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1389">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Gao, H., Kadirjan-Kalbach, D., Froehlich, J. E. &amp; Osteryoung, K. W. ARC5, a cytosolic dynamin-like protein from plants, is part of the chloroplast division machinery. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 100, 4328–4333 (2003)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1392">16</a></sup>. With mitochondrial divisions, however, whilst dynamin rings are retained in higher organisms, FtsZ and MD rings are not clearly observed, and it is possible that they were replaced by other systems during eukaryotic evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Miyagishima, S., Nishida, K. &amp; Kuroiwa, T. An evolutionary puzzle: chloroplast and mitochondrial division rings. Trends Plant Sci. 8, 432–438 (2003)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1397">13</a></sup>. MD/PD ring genes are yet unknown, although their identification should be accelerated by works such as this. Although the microbody, a single-membrane-bounded organelle, divides by binary fission in <i>C. merolae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Miyagishima, S. et al. Microbody proliferation and segregation cycle in the single-microbody alga Cyanidioschyzon merolae. Planta 208, 326–336 (1999)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1403">17</a></sup>, it lacks Pex11p, which is a known key regulator of microbody division and proliferation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Marshall, P. A. et al. Pmp27 promotes peroxisomal proliferation. J. Cell Biol. 129, 345–355 (1995)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1407">18</a></sup>.</p><p>The following proteins related to cell motility and cytokinesis were encoded in <i>C. merolae</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 5</a>); one set of tubulin, two actins, five proteins of the kinesin family, and several intermediate filament proteins. However, no genes encoding myosin or proteins containing dynein motor domains were found. The absence of the myosin gene is consistent with the fact that electron microscopy and immuno-detection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Takahashi, H. et al. A possible role of actin dots in the formation of the contractile ring in the ultra-micro alga Cyanidium caldarium RK-1. Protoplasma 201, 115–119 (1998)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1420">19</a></sup> techniques did not detect microfilaments of actin; cDNA clones for actin genes were also not obtained. In the red alga <i>Cyanidium caldarium</i> RK-1,which is closely related to <i>C. merolae</i> but has a genome double the size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Toda, K., Takahashi, H., Itoh, R. &amp; Kuroiwa, T. DNA contents of cell nuclei in two Cyanidiophyceae: Cyanidioschyzon merolae and Cyanidium caldarium Forma A. Cytologia (Tokyo) 60, 183–188 (1995)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1431">7</a></sup>, cells divide using a contractile ring of actin filaments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Takahashi, H. et al. A possible role of actin dots in the formation of the contractile ring in the ultra-micro alga Cyanidium caldarium RK-1. Protoplasma 201, 115–119 (1998)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1435">19</a></sup>. <i>C. merolae</i> cells therefore seem to divide using a system that is simpler than that of actomyosin.</p><p><i>C. merolae</i> has noteworthy properties, which are relevant for examining the origin of eukaryotes, and primary and secondary plastid endosymbiosis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Nozaki, H. et al. The phylogenetic position of red algae revealed by multiple nuclear genes from mitochondria-containing eukaryotes and an alternative hypothesis on the origin of plastids. J. Mol. Evol. 56, 485–497 (2003)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1447">20</a></sup>. Only 30 transfer RNAs (tRNAs) were detected in the nuclear genome using the program tRNAscan-SE with relaxed parameter settings (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>). Some of these tRNA genes showed possible archaeal features, namely, ectopic introns and anticodon GAU for tRNA-Ile (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). Four of these tRNA genes seemed to have introns in the D-loop region, whereas the introns of eukaryotic tRNA genes are limited to a site 3′ to the anticodon. As ectopic tRNA introns have been reported in some archaeal genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Marck, C. &amp; Grosjean, H. tRNomics: analysis of tRNA genes from 50 genomes of Eukarya, Archaea, and Bacteria reveals anticodon-sparing strategies and domain-specific features. RNA 8, 1189–1232 (2002)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1457">21</a></sup>, this could explain the paucity of detected tRNA genes in <i>C. merolae</i>; tRNAscan-SE might have overlooked other tRNAs owing to the existence of unknown types of ectopic introns. Another point to note is that <i>C. merolae</i> possesses a single tRNA-Ile with anticodon GAU, which has not been observed in eukaryotes, but only in prokaryotes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Marck, C. &amp; Grosjean, H. tRNomics: analysis of tRNA genes from 50 genomes of Eukarya, Archaea, and Bacteria reveals anticodon-sparing strategies and domain-specific features. RNA 8, 1189–1232 (2002)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1468">21</a></sup>.</p><p>Standard sets of photosystem genes, including those encoding phycobilisome components, were observed in <i>C. merolae</i>. Many of them (11 PSI genes and 17 PSII genes) are encoded in the plastid genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Ohta, N. et al. Complete sequence and analysis of the plastid genome of the unicellular red alga Cyanidioschyzon merolae. DNA Res. 10, 67–77 (2003)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1478">22</a></sup>, while PsbO, P, U, Z as well as a distant PsbQ homologue are encoded in the nuclear genome. Although only PsbU and PsbZ were previously identified in red algal PSII, the localization of PsbP and putative PsbQ in PSII, as recently suggested in <i>Synechocystis</i> sp. PCC6803<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Kashino, Y. et al. Proteomic analysis of a highly active photosystem II preparation from the cyanobacterium Synechocystis sp. PCC 6803 reveals the presence of novel polypeptides. Biochemistry 41, 8004–8012 (2002)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1485">23</a></sup> is an interesting subject of proteomic study. The genes <i>psaH, N, X</i>, as well as <i>psbS</i> and <i>ndh</i> genes are not encoded in either the plastid or the nuclear genomes. Therefore, the photosystems of <i>C. merolae</i> lack various mechanisms for dissipating excessive light energy.</p><p>Enzymes of the Calvin cycle in plants are known to be a mosaic of enzymes originating from cyanobacteria-like ancestors of an endosymbiont and its eukaryotic host<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Martin, W. &amp; Schnarrenberger, C. The evolution of the Calvin cycle from prokaryotic to eukaryotic chromosomes: a case study of functional redundancy in ancient pathways through endosymbiosis. Curr. Genet. 32, 1–18 (1997)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1505">24</a></sup>. Red algal ribulose-1,5-bisphosphate carboxylase/oxygenase (Rubisco) is known to be a product of horizontal gene transfer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ohta, N., Sato, N., Ueda, K. &amp; Kuroiwa, T. Analysis of a plastid gene cluster reveals a close relationship between Cyanidioschyzon and Cyanidium. J. Plant Res. 110, 235–245 (1997)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1509">25</a></sup>. The origin of other Calvin cycle enzymes is essentially identical in <i>C. merolae</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 6</a>). It is highly probable that the complex and mosaic origin of Calvin cycle enzymes derived from common ancestors of green plants and red algae, and no essential changes occurred after the separation of the two lineages. This is strong support for the concept of a single event of primary plastid endosymbiosis. Among the known translocon proteins of plastids, Toc34, Toc75, Tic20, Tic22 and Tic110 were encoded in the <i>C. merolae</i> genome, but other proteins such as Toc159, Tic40 and Tic55 were not found. Results of phylogenetic analysis of the five translocon components (to be published elsewhere) also suggest the concept<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Cavalier-Smith, T. Genomic reduction and evolution of novel genetic membranes and protein-targeting machinery in eukaryote–eukaryote chimaeras (meta-algae). Phil. Trans. R. Soc. Lond. B 358, 109–134 (2003)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1529">26</a></sup>.</p><p>Another aspect of the comparative genomics of the red algal genome is secondary endosymbiosis. Cryptophytes are thought to retain a remnant of the endosymbiotic red algal nucleus, the nucleomorph, in the periplastidic compartment. The sequencing of the cryptophyte alga <i>Guillardia theta</i> nucleomorph genome revealed a number of curious architectural features that might be shared by the genome of red algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Douglas, S. et al. The highly reduced genome of an enslaved algal nucleus. Nature 40, 1091–1096 (2001)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1540">27</a></sup>. <i>C. merolae</i> chromosomes showed multiple subtelomeric duplications, but did not contain rRNA gene clusters such as those of the nucleomorph genome. This implies that the telomeric rRNA gene clusters observed in the nucleomorph genome, as well as other prominent genome structures such as overlapped genes, appeared after secondary symbiosis. It is also notable that ectopic tRNA introns are also reported in nucleomorph tRNAs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zauner, S. et al. Chloroplast protein and centrosomal genes, a tRNA intron, and odd telomeres in an unusually compact eukaryotic genome, the cryptomonad nucleomorph. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 97, 200–205 (2000)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1547">28</a></sup>. Details of the comparisons with the nucleomorph genome will be presented elsewhere.</p><p>Light signal transduction is critical for the growth and differentiation of photoautotrophic organisms. As the division of <i>C. merolae</i> cells is synchronized by light, an elaborate mechanism for light signal transduction must exist. Several putative blue light receptor (cryptochrome) genes were found in <i>C. merolae</i>, whereas no genes encoding phytochromes and phototropins were identified. As bacterial phytochrome genes are only found in some species of cyanobacteria with large genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Montgomery, B. L. &amp; Lagarias, J. C. Phytochrome ancestry: sensors of bilins and light. Trends Plant Sci. 7, 357–366 (2002)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1560">29</a></sup>, the ancestor of plastids might be an ancestral cyanobacterium without phytochromes. This also suggests that the phytochromes of higher plants might not be of cyanobacterial origin. In higher plants, various signalling pathways (such as the two-component system consisting of histidine kinases and response regulators as well as a MAP kinase cascade) are involved in the signal transduction of various hormones, and in the development of organs. In <i>C. merolae</i>, the presence of only a single candidate for histidine kinase and a dozen MAP kinase-related molecules is suggested. However, there are no response regulators other than those that are plastid-encoded, trimeric G protein and adenylate cyclase. Thus, <i>C. merolae</i> appear to use only a limited repertoire of signal transduction mechanisms, which corroborates the lack of cell differentiation in this alga.</p><p><i>C. merolae</i> is an alga in which all of the three genome compartments—nucleus, mitochondrion (32,211 bp)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Ohta, N., Sato, N. &amp; Kuroiwa, T. Structure and organization of the mitochondrial genome of the unicellular red alga Cyanidioschyzon merolae deduced from the complete nucleotide sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 26, 5190–5198 (1998)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1575">30</a></sup> and plastid (149,987 bp)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Ohta, N. et al. Complete sequence and analysis of the plastid genome of the unicellular red alga Cyanidioschyzon merolae. DNA Res. 10, 67–77 (2003)" href="/articles/nature02398#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d3545e1579">22</a></sup>—have been sequenced. Such information is a prerequisite for future studies on proteomics, expression analysis using microarrays, and structural biology with heat-stable proteins that are unique among eukaryotes. All of this information will, in turn, help elucidate the origin, evolution and fundamental mechanisms of the single- as well as double-membrane-bounded organelles, and ultimately all photosynthetic eukaryotes. In addition, this hot spring alga will be useful in analysing the mechanisms of heat and acid tolerance in eukaryotic cells.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Whole genome shotgun sequencing</h3><p>We sequenced the <i>C. merolae</i> genome by the whole genome random sequencing method (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> for details). About 335,000 insert ends were sequenced, which covered the genome 11 times. BAC libraries with two subsets were constructed and a large-scale full-length cDNA library from cells cultured under various growth conditions prepared. The sequences were assembled using Phrap, further examined by referring to another assembly using ARACHNE, and edited using CONSED. The scaffolds were built within the hybridization groups using read-pair information from the BAC, shotgun and cDNA clones. The gaps between the contigs were closed by primer walking PCR, and mate-pair clone and BAC clone sequencings.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Gene identification and annotation</h3><p>We principally used two strategies for gene prediction and combined the results (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nature02398#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> for details). (1) Each read-pair of cDNA clones was mapped on the contigs using BLAST and putatively transcribed regions were determined by clustering the mapped pairs. (2) ORFs likely to encode a protein showing similarity to known proteins or having known motifs were identified respectively by the BLASTP program with GenBank nr database, or a HMMER program with a Pfam database. A functional classification was performed based on the NCBI KOG. The tRNA genes were detected using the tRNAscan-SE program with relaxed parameters (-X 15 -I -36).</p></div></div></section>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 8 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=The%20COG%20database%3A%20an%20updated%20version%20includes%20eukaryotes&amp;journal=BMC%20Bioinform.&amp;volume=4&amp;publication_year=2003&amp;author=Tatusov%2CRL">
                    Google Scholar</a> 
                </p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="A.
